Admin istra tion
ADMINISTRATOR S MESSAGE
This Rosh Hashanah, the advent of the Jewish year, marks the
advent of my fifth year as your Administrator.
My efforts have been directed to contribute in some measure to
the growth of the Center and to emphasize the importance of the
Synagogue in the lives of the Center Family.
The advice, counsel and friendship of the Officers and Mem¬
bers of the Board have guided me in executing our Synagogue
policies.
My thanks are especially extended to the "Administrative” com¬
mittee chairmen and committeemen whose areas of responsibility
are outlined below.
The valued opinions and advice of the other members of our
staff, Cantor Marton, Mr. Gross and Mr. Fischer have made it
possible to implement successfully the Center’s religions, educa¬
tional and youth programs.
To carry out the myriad of details of Center administration,
my appreciation is extended to the efficient and friendly office
personnel, Mrs. Ann Stegman, Mrs. Florence Robin, Mrs. Esther
Fishier and Mr. Kenneth Bowden.
For the proper maintenance of our physical plant my thanks
go to Timothy Riley, Morris Montford, Eugene Davis, Wallace
Mazon and Anna Mae McNeil.
DAVID WEINSTEIN
Administrator
Just as each and every member feels the loss of our beloved
Rabbi Tofield, of blessed memory, so do I deeply miss his wise
counsel, warmth and religious inspiration.
As your Administrator I look forward this coming year to the
many rewarding experiences which I derive from my association
with the leaders and members of the Center Family.
Mrs. Ann Stegman
Secretary
Mrs. Florence Robin
Secretary
Mr. Kenneth Bowden
Comptroller
Our loyal maintenance staff, Front row: Wallace Mazon, Annie
Mae McNeil, Timothy Riley
Back row: George Shaver, Eugene Davis, Morris Montford.

DR. JACK ROSNER,

Vi WHMi
NORMAN MOSS, CW.
Holiday Ushering
Committee
The Finance Committee is responsible for the development of
sources of income for an increased educational and youth program.
The success of Center growth requires the financial cooperation of the
entire membership in response to the efforts of the Finance Committee.
Makes careful analysis of the financial status of the Center. The
goal of the committee is to develop the most progressive programming
and balance the resources and expenditures for the coming year.
By constant revision of house rules, regulations and service fees
improves public relations to the mutual benefit of our membership and
the Center. The beauty and cleanliness of the Center premises are the
result of our capable and devoted maintenance staff.
Sets as its goal to acquaint the entire Jewish community with the
religious, educational and youth programs which are sponsored by the
Center. To accomplish these goals, it is constantly canvassing prospects
among newcomers, newly married couples, unaffiliated Jewish families
and young single adults. The entire membership can assist this com¬
mittee by informing them of prospective members.
Has as its task the proper furnishing of the Center. With funds
supplied by the Beautification Committee of the Sisterhood the most
beautiful and durable articles are chosen to enhance the appearance
of the building’s interior.
Supervises and directs the planning of building expansion. The
growth of the Center Family has necessitated constant increase of our
facilities. The efforts of this committee are directed to fulfill these needs
in our most economic manner in keeping with the beauty and dignity
of a Synagogue-Center.
JOE BECKER, Chmn.
Budget Committee
SID MACK, Chmn,
Affiliations Committee
RALPH MIZRAHI, Chmn,
Architect’s Committee
Seeks to enhance the High Holiday Services by quietly and quickly seating the wor¬
shippers. Their constant effort over the years has greatly improved the decorum of the
congregation which has added greater dignity to the services.

Scope of Activities
Jacksonville Jewish Center
The Conservative Synagogue in Jacksonville
Our Year Book reviews the programs and activities we make available for religious inspiration, education
benefit of new members and newcomers to the city, we set forth our program in brief.
We call attention that the name "Center” is to be distinguished from organizations by similar names in
primarily recreational Centers.
We are a rfSynagogue-Center
RELIGIOUS SERVICES
As a Conservative Synagogue our religious services include daily morning and evening minyans as well as the traditional observance of
the Sabbath and Festivals.
High Holidays: Members and their families, service-men and out-of-town guests are accommodated in our air-conditioned Auditorium
which seats 1400. (We do not "sell seats,” as the expression goes in some communities.)
PERSONAL OBSERVANCES
Our formal public worship services are supplemented by the guidance of our religious leadership on personal occasions at the syna¬
gogue and home. They are the observances of the customs in connection with Circumcision, naming of girls, Pidyon Ha-ben, Bar
Mitzvah, Bas Mitzvah, Confirmations, Marriages, Conversion to Judaism, Bereavement.
and Jewish living. For the
other communities that are
EDUCATION
Daily Kindergarten: Mon. to Fri., 9 to 12 noon., p. 83
Week-day Hebrew School: P-78, Classes for boys and girls on alternate afternoons of the week in three divisions:
1. Beginners —• 7 to 8 yrs. of age
2. Elementary Grades, extensive and intensive divisions corresponding to Grades 3-8.
3. Advanced Grades, 9 and 10 intensive only.
Sunday Religious School: Sun. mornings 9 to 12 noon; pp. 71, 73, 75
Pre-Kindergarten through Grade 11 and Confirmation Class.
Jr. Congregation: Ages 11 to 13, p. 81

Library: Books available for young and old in the new school bldg., p. 77
Adult Courses:
Lecture series are presented as available. Institutes on specific subjects are arranged in convenient units of study. Special classes
and discussion groups are arranged as called for.

===== PAGE 17 =====
This Year Book is dedicated to Dr. Sanders A. Tofield,
our beloved Rabbi of blessed memory, of whom it may be
said, "Which is the right course that a man should choose
for himself? That which he feels to be honorable to him¬
self, and which also brings him honor from mankind."
(Ethics of the Fathers)
Leadership requires vision; religious leadership needs
the added qualities of faith and dedication.
The Rabbi proclaimed these ideals, "With faith in
God's help, with confidence in the eternal verity of our
work, in the interests of achieving profounder happiness for
ourselves and for our children, let us go forward with the
progress that is within reach!" (Year Book 1947). Again
after his many years of service in his 1959 message, "Let us
reflect on the God-given blessing of life and the periods of
time in which it is realized. Let us hold sacred the year
ahead and step into it with spiritual dedication."
The Jewish people, the people of the Book, have al¬
ways valued and respected knowledge. The entire congre¬
gation took pride when the Doctor of Hebrew Letters was
conferred on him in 1954. An outstanding Talmudic
scholar, a member of the Law Committee of the Rabbinical
Assembly, the Rabbi was sensitive to the need for adapting
modern-day observance of Judaism within the framework
of Jewish tradition.
This philosophy was expressed, "But it is quite evident
that the era of experimentation and adaptation is coming to
a close. The Jacksonville Jewish Center is acquiring a
galaxy of Conservative Jewish congregations in America.
Our program receives direction from the past and zest from
the needs of the present." (Year Book 1947)
Not only was he respected nationally but was beloved
by the many rabbis with whom he was intimately associ¬
ated and many whom he guided in his capacity as the Presi¬
dent of the Southeast Region of the Rabbinical Assembly.
His interest in Israel and Zionism was recognized by
his colleagues when he was appointed for a special mission
by the Rabbinical Assembly to Israel. He sent his greet¬
ings to us from Jerusalem in 1949, "The same blessing l
RABBI SANDERS A. TOFIELD
extend to you, my friends back home, for the coming New
Year: 'The Lord bless thee out of Zion;
And see thou the good of Jerusalem all the days of
thy life;
And see thy childrens children, Peace be upon
Israel!' " (1949 Year Book)
In our own congregation his efforts were especially di¬
rected to the welfare of the children. He constantly strived
to set the highest standards of religious education in our
school. The growth and success can easily be seen in the
camparison of his first and last Confirmation Classes.
It was through his impetus that the U.S.Y. was formed
RABBI RECEIVING HIS DOCTORATE DEGREE
RABBI WITH SISTERHOOD MEMBERS
AT A CONVENTION

CONFIRMATION CLASSES

quainted and active with our own young people and those
of the rest of the Southeast Region. Prominent in Scout¬
ing circles, he took a great deal of pride in preparing
Scouts for the Ner Tamid award. In 1959 our troop had
the largest award winning group (14).
The Sisterhood, Men’s Club and all affiliated organiza¬
tions, as well as the community at large, benefited from his
active interest in their affairs. "Recorded here is also the
contribution made to American Jewry by other associations.
Each one forms, as it tv ere, another column supporting the
edipce that Israel is building in an American outpost. The
structure of world Jewry is thereby magnified. The honor
or Israel is kept aloft, hi most instances the Center renders
direct service to the organizations listed. With all, it main¬
tains an affinity of interest. To all, it extends cooperation
and recognition as noteworthy endeavors and pledges that
his own expanded facilities will prove to their enhance¬
ment
With the leaders of the congregation he was in the
forefront of all the building expansion programs. Our Sanc¬
tuary with its beautiful Ark was the result of his inspira¬
tional conception. The Youth Building and new Education
Building were the vehicles whereby the youth and education
programs were implemented.
With his beloved wife and daughter and family as his
constant companions, the family was an example of ideal
Jewish family life.
His Center Family will best remember him for his
comforting presence in the time of bereavement, his en¬
couragement to those ill and in pain. Those with personal
problems benefited from his wise counsel. He imparted
the wisdom of the Torah in his adult education groups.
In every way and at all times he established a warm
intimacy with every man, woman and child.
We offer the prayer in his behalf as it is said "He in
whom the spirit of his fellow creatures takes delight, in
him the Spirit of the All present takes delight

===== PAGE 21 =====
Message by
Rabbi Rosenberg
• |
In the Year Book of last year, Rabbi Sanders A.
Tofield wrote, “No one year is like another year.”
j
Indeed it isn’t! As we usher in the New Year of
5721, the beloved Rabbi of the Center is of “Blessed
Memory! ”
In a real sense, however, Rabbi Tofield lives and
his blessed memory shall always endure! His noble
spirit, his dedicated leadership, his devoted service
permeate throughout the entire Jacksonville Jewish
Center as well as throughout the community as a
whole.
The respect and reverence which all people had
for him must continue to manifest itself. It must be
RABBI YAAKOV ROSENBERG
GUEST RABBI
FOR THE HIGH HOLIDAYS
reflected in dedicating yourselves with ever increasing
devotion to those ideals and institutions for which
he gave of himself so indefatigably.
As we greet 5721, let us pledge that the Center
will continue to grow as that spiritual “Powerhouse”
from which Torah and learning, ethics and morals,
love of Torah—the land the people, devotion to our
America and its ideals, and conscious worship of
God will become daily realities in the lives of all the
Center families, in a world of universal brotherhood
and everlasting peace. AMEN.

===== PAGE 23 =====
President s Message
It is an honor for me to be able to greet all of our Center
members and friends through this Year Book, which contains
the history of our congregation during the past year.
Unfortunately, we must in this issue record the death of our
late rabbi, Dr. Sanders A. Tofield "of blessed memory". Our
congregation was the beneficiary of his devoted and selfless
service for thirteen years. We inherited from him a deep appre¬
ciation of the high ethical and moral principles of our faith.
It is incumbent upon our congregation to keep alive the mem¬
ory of Rabbi Tofield by incorporating into our lives the Love of
Torah which was so deeply imbedded in his life. The congrega¬
tion’s dedication of the Tofield Memorial Library will be a last¬
ing reminder to us of his zeal for learning and his zest for
teaching.
It is imperative that our congregation remains true to the
ideals that Rabbi Tofield deemed so important, and which are
the cornerstones of our institution, named "Torah — Avodah —
G1 miles Che sed" which represent Study—Worship—Doing of
Good Deeds.
LOUIS SAFER, President
Your administration will work harder than ever to continue
the meaningful program which Rabbi Tofield organized and nurtured. Our staff has assumed greater responsibility than ever before.
I am confident that our membership will continue its loyal support of all our Center activities.
On behalf of the Officers, Directors and Trustees, I extend to each of you best wishes on the occasion of this New Year. May
we all be blessed with good health, spiritual enlightenment, peace and prosperity. May the coming year crown with success all our
efforts and aspirations. "Le Shanah Tovah Tikatevu"

BUILDING FUND
Now that our school building has been remodeled and the rear
portion of the Synogogue building completely remodeled for more
than a year, the Building Fund requirements of the Center take on
an entirely different aspect.
We are no longer faced with the necessity of providing addi¬
tional building facilities. Instead we are faced with maintaining
what we have in the finest possible condition.
The Building Fund is therefore primarily interested in the fol¬
lowing immediate goals and aims:
1. First, we must continue to meet our mortgage payments as
they come due. At the same time we must begin to devise plans for
retiring these mortgages within the next five years.
2. We must maintain the facilities we have in a first class con¬
dition in order to protect our investment and to get the maximum
use from them and to protect our investment.
3. We must have funds for capital replacements. For example,
we must replace some of our present equipment.
4. We must provide certain facilities as they become needed,
such as:
(a) Increasing our air conditioning tonnage from 80 to at
least 200 tons.
(b) Paving of the parking lot property.
(c ) Provision of certain minor outdoor recreational facili¬
ties etc.
The Building Committee has set up a five-year program which
will provide all of the above goals as well as insure the retirement
of all mortgage indebtedness of the Synogague within a period of
five years. We hope that such a program as the committee has now
adopted will not be too difficult to get across to our members, just
because they do not see any visible signs of construction activities.
ROBERT J. GORDON
The progress made during the past ten years is solely due to the
generosity of a goodly percentage of our membership plus an ex¬
cellent credit reputation. At the present time, this is our most valu¬
able asset and must be maintained at all costs, This can only be
accomplished through the support and cooperation of the members
who have continued to make annual pledges to our Building Fund.
It is possible some of our members may feel we have pressured
them too much during the past year and trust the above informa¬
tion will explain the reason for this pressure.
The writer is indeed grateful to the many devoted and generous
individuals who have responded to these appeals and have helped
overcome many critical situations. I ask for your continued cooper¬
ation which will enable us to maintain our credit standing.
Our best wishes for a Happy and a Good New Year. "Le Sha-
nah Tovah Tikatevu.”
Mr. Harry Gendzior presenting a plaque to Mr. Robert J. Gordon
for outstanding service.

Center Sisterhood
President
Vice-President, Cultural
Vice-President, Youth
Vice-President, Fellowship

Social Secretary

Treasurer
Mrs. Bert Meyer

Mrs. Morris Price
... Mrs. William Rose
Mrs. Moe Zager
Mrs. Herbert Lewitt
Mrs. Henry Bettman
..Mrs. I. P. Edwards
Mrs. Melvin Hollins
MRS. BERT MEYER
The 565 women who are members of Sisterhood are proud of the important part that they play in the Center’s program and growth.
Sisterhood functions as a vital organ of the many Center activities, working to aid its educational, recreational and spiritual programs.
One highlight of the Sisterhood’s program this past year was the November Cabaret. It was overwhelmingly successful, attracting
groups from every segment of the Center Family. Because the response was so rewarding, another dance has been planned for this fall,
which will use a Hawaiian theme.
The outstanding Sisterhood project of the year was the Beautification drive which culminated in the Donors event. A record $4,000.00
was raised which this year went into desks and chairs for the Hebrew and Sunday School. This furnishings project being almost com¬
plete, Sisterhood looks forward to raising money for the completion of the Daily Chapel and the Youth Building Foyer.
Projects this year included: Providing salaries of the teachers in the Sunday Religious School, prizes and awards for pupils of the
Hebrew and Sunday Schools, refreshments for special celebrations, giving financial assistance as well as advisory supervision to the Center
Youth League and the Girl Scouts. The Sisterhood also provided needed equipment such as a commercial vacuum cleaner and an electric
typewriter.
Sisterhood is affiliated with the National Women’s League and this year sent delegates to the Regional Conference of the Southeast
Branch of United Synagogue in Miami, Fla. This affiliation provides guidance and information in fulfilling local programs. Sisterhood
members enjoyed a variety of programs at meetings during the year which included a book review, musical program, children’s style
show and an original skit. The programs which are produced by members of the Sisterhood serve the purpose of entertainment, enlighten¬
ment and enthusiasm for the goals set by Sisterhood.
The Gift Shop had an outstanding year and was instrumental in fulfilling Sisterhood’s objective of strengthening Judaism and mak¬
ing it more meaningful through the Synagogue and the home.

EDWIN PRESSER
OFFICERS
President Edwin Presser

Membership Co-Chairmen—

Top Picture: Winners of Beauty contest at Center Sunday School
Picnic—Sandra Shmunes, Susan Hilsenrad and Lynelle Resnick.
Middle Picture: Winners in Beauty contest—Deborah Worman,

The Officers and Directors of the Center Men’s Club are proud to report to its members and friends that the 1959-60 season was
one of great progress and accomplishment. A representative list of services rendered our community under an able and active group of
leaders and with the full support of our members, includes: Sunday Minyan Club Breakfasts, full Camp Ramah Scholarships, coopera¬
tion with Synagogue and Religious School departments, Religious School awards, Sunday School Picnic, monthly meetings and dinners,
community service award, Boy Scout award, Sunday School baseball trip, Blood Bank, Bowling League.
Our annual Sunday School Picnic, held on the last day of Sunday School at the Adolph Wurn Park in the Sans Souci area, was
met with acclaim and approbation, the youngsters having a wonderful time and taking home many awards. The attendance at Sunday
Minyan Club Breakfasts has swelled gratifyingly, and our Blood Bank project, open to all members of the community, completed a very
successful year.
We have begun two new projects this past year. The Center Men’s Club Bowling League had a full season, and climaxed its first
year with a dinner-dance at the Beauclerc Club, at which time awards were given to top teams and members.
Providing Camp Ramah Scholarships has been taken on as a principal project of the club and we were able to provide a full scholar¬
ship for a meritorious student of our Religious School.
The Center Men’s Club has shown itself to be a very vital integral part of our Jewish community and we are striving to widen and
improve our services to you and your children. We look forward with anticipation to many more successful years to come and urge your
support through membership and activity.
Anyone interested in affiliation should contact any of the Officers or come to our regularly scheduled dinner meetings.
Men’s Club members serving the Minyan Club Breakfast

Camp Ramah
The Camp Ramah movement provides our youth with a unique
summer camp experience. It is a dynamic and unique program de¬
signed to enrich educationally, religiously, culturally and socially
the lives of boys and girls in the Conservative Movement.
Each phase of the camp’s program is most unusual.
Devoted and dedicated laymen composing the Board of Direc¬
tors have the responsibility of setting the camp’s policies.
It is said of the faculty of over 400 men and women who ad¬
minister the camp to more than 1400 boys and girls that a sum¬
mer at Ramah is more than a job—it is a calling, a way of life, a
way in which to teach boys and girls to walk in the paths of God.
Children pray in Hebrew and play in Hebrew. The maxim "The
study of the law is equal in value to all else” is practiced by cam¬
pers, Directors, Counsellors and the athletic specialist.
Jewish observance, based in the framework of traditional prac¬
tice but interpreted to our modern way of life, is practiced daily—
the entire camp "davans” three times a day, make the blessings be¬
fore and after meals, sing Zemirot in celebration of the Sabbath—
religion is meaningful and relevant in the daily routine of all.
And just as important—that which makes a camp experience
adventurous and exciting—swimming, athletics, nature study, arts
PHILIP SELBER
and crafts, dancing, dramatics, all taught by specialists; in campsites
with the most modern facilities; in the beauty spots of the nation;
in the Poconos, in Connecticut, Canada, California and Wisconsin.
In the words of Reuben R. Kaufman, "We who are concerned
with the creative life of American Jewry must make it our task to
develop into righteous people as many boys and girls and young
men and young women as we can find—and help! It is to this
proposition that Ramah is dedicated and, with your help, Ramah
will go from strength to strength!”

Jewish Theological Seminary
WHAT THE JEWISH THEOLOGICAL SEMINARY OF AMERICA
MEANS TO YOU AND YOUR FAMILY
The Jewish Theological Seminary of America in New York is an academy of
learning which keeps alive the values and traditions of Judaism symbolized by
the Eternal Light. It is a fountainhead—for through its teachers, scholars, and
leaders come the dynamic interpretation of Judaism that inspires, furthers creative
Jewish life and encourages inter-group understanding.
A museum exhibit furnished by the Seminary
Dr. Alexander Marx, Dr. Finkelstein
& Dr. Lieberman
Dr. Louis Finkelstein, Chancellor of Semi¬
nary, with Rabbi Tofield.
AT THE SEMINARY
Rabbis are trained in the Rabbinical School to transmit the heritage of Juda¬
ism to congregations in the United States and Canada.
The world’s largest collection of Jewish books and manuscripts helps scholar
and layman to understand Jewish ideals.
Students in the Teachers’ Institute are trained to make Jewish learning an
exciting experience for our children.
Textbooks, programs guides, regional educational conferences, teacher and
cantor placements are made through the United Synagogue of America.
Leaders of all faiths plan the course of study for the Institute of Religious
and Social Studies.
An unusual and interesting Jewish Museum offers unlimited studies.
Ramah Camps for youngsters of Jewish environment, combining the physical
with the spiritual.
At the Seminary comes the planning for the award-winning Eternal Light
radio program, the Frontiers of Faith on Television and other programs which
reach millions.
Our Congregation Seminary Chairmen, Mr. Jack Becker and Mr. Philip Bork,
are charged with the responsibility of collecting the funds for supporting all
these activities.

with gold and silver threads on ruby velvet
ground.

Several significant improvements were introduced this year and innovations adopted
previously were strengthened.
Our Hebrew School furthered its objective of classifying pupils into compatible age
groups and scholastic ability. Hebrew School classes have almost become parallel with
public school classes. Two sessions each day and extensive and intensive divisions make th s
possible. With the help of an additional full time professional teacher, we were able to in¬
stitute an integrated program for nine year old beginners who meet with the same teacher
three times a week. This year’s beginners’ class thus numbers 72 pupils. Among the inno¬
vations is the 8th grade Hebrew School class that meets from 5:00 to 7:30 p.m. with a half
hour for supper served in our Cafeteria.
Jr. Congregation and Pre-Jr. Congregation attendance at Sabbath Services continued
to grow and the unit leader system introduced last year has proved most helpful.
On Sunday mornings following Minyan Club services the breakfasts have been put on
a self-sustaining basis, each boy pays $1.50 a month. The Men’s Club continues to cater the
breakfast, its financial resources being directed to other projects like Camp Ramah scholar¬
ships.
The new Classroom Building in addition to fulfilling its primary function has enabled
us this year to re-activate the Arts and Crafts department and initiate a full scale Library
program. The Assembly Hall and convertible Chapel helped immeasurably with children’s
services.

Ritual and Worship
As this Year Book goes to press the Jacksonville Jewish Center is like a ship without
its captain. Shortly after Shavuot our beloved Rabbi, Dr. Sanders A. Tofield, was called to
his Eternal Reward. May his memory ever be a source of inspiration and a blessing to us.
The years of Rabbi’s guidance and indoctrination produced a goodly seasoned crew of
staff, officers and board members for the Center. This training stood us in good stead during
Rabbi’s illness and since his passing. Relying heavily upon the charts and instructions Rabbi
left us, we were able to sail the good ship Center through the turbulent seas of year 5720.
All of our services, daily, Sabbath and Holiday have continued to adhere as closely as pos¬
sible to those of previous years.
The daily Minyans have been strengthened by members of the Board who volunteered
to observe the Kaddish for Rabbi. The Sabbath services are led by laymen or by guest
rabbis when available. The attendance at these services continues good.
For the High Holidays this year we were most fortunate to have secured the services
af Rabbi Yaakov Rosenberg. We look forward to his messages of inspiration. As in past
years Aliyahs and Honors will be distributed as fairly and as widely as possible throughout
our membership.
NATHAN FREIDLIN
Our goal remains as always to promote and encourage attendance and participation in all our services.
We pray the Almighty send us a new captain soon to guide us in the spiritual forces of our tradition towards greater love and re¬
spect for the Jewish way of life. AMEN
The Holy Ark in Our Synagogue

Youth Activities
YOUTH ACTIVITIES
With a full time Director for the Activities Department,
we have been able to provide a well rounded program for all
ages. Utilizing all of our facilities we now offer the following:
CENTER YOUTH ORGANIZATIONS
For High School students—a U.S.Y. Chapter that func¬
tions with the National Organization, meeting bi-weekly with
a varied program; Explorer Scouts organized for boys who
wish to continue their Boy Scout training; Center Youth
Board composed of presidents of all the youth clubs meeting
at the Center that acts as a governing body for all youth
problems.
For Junior High School—a similar U.S.Y. group for
the younger teen-agers also affiliated with the national move¬
ment of U.S.Y.; Boy Scout Troops for this same age group
meeting weekly with many extra activities such as hikes, out¬
ings and jamborees.
For 5th, 6th and 7th Graders—Social clubs with monthly
functions; Boy Scouts and Girl Scouts.
For the Younger Set—Brownie and Cub Scout programs
affiliated with the local Scout headquarters.
Youth activities sponsored by the Center include teen-age
canteens with music, refreshment and floor shows; athletics
for boys and girls in all sports and a bowling league for teen¬
agers.
Charles Palot Youth Fund
This Fund finances all transportation charges to the Region¬
al U.S.Y. Conventions and the annual Leadership Training
Institute for C.Y.L. and C.P.C. members. The soul source of
income is through donations tothis Fund.
BOB FISCHER

CENTER YOUTH LEAGUE
Chapter of United Synagogue Youth
President Rita Diamond
1st Vice-President Byron Kolitz
2nd Vice-President Judy Safer
Recording Secretary Marsha Kramer
Corresponding Secy Donna Hackel and Evelyn Brauner
Treasurer Simon Selber
Advisor Stanley Kantor
STANLEY KANTOR RITA DIAMOND
The 1959-60 school year was one of the most successful that C.Y.L. has had since its organization. Over 55 high school teen¬
agers took part in its activities.
Our delegations to the U.S.Y. conventions during the year included the following: Leadership Training Institute at Camp Blue
Star in Hendersonville—15 delegates; one delegate to National Convention in New York where Stephen Entman was elected Na¬
tional Treasurer; Regional Convention in Birmingham during Feb.—29 delegates.
_ t
Stewart Goldberg was elected Regional Treasurer, while several other members were appointed regional chairmen. We were also
represented at the Regional Board Meeting in Hollywood by four delegates, accompanied by Mr. and Mrs. Bob Fischer.
A few highlights during the past year were: telephone interview with Eddie Cantor in Hollywood and the Israeli Ambassador
to the U.S. in Washington; piano concert by Gerson Yessin, Jacksonville University professor; a report by Chamber of Commerce
representative on his current trip to Russia.
Some of the new projects started this year that proved very successful were: Monthly Friday night service attendance followed
by a discussion on current Jewish stopics, a weekly U.S.Y. girls’ bowling league and monthly visit to River Garden Home for the Aged.
To climax the year at our installation banquet, the annual awards for outstanding boy and girl in C.Y.L. were given to Stewart
Goldberg and Janet Goldstein.
Our sincerest appreciation goes to our advisor Stanley Kantor, Sisterhood Youth Chairman Mrs. Louis Slott and our Youth Activities
Director Bob Fischer for the help and understanding they extended us.
MAJOR S PRE- TEENS
INSTALLATION
BANQUET
President Peggy Cohen
Vice-President Gail Price
Vice-President Frances Lipson
Secretary Stacy Wolf
Treasurer Cheryl Sussman
A successful year was concluded by the Major’s Pre-Teens,
under the capable guidance of the Mothers’ Committee and the
sponsors. This group is for boys and girls in the seventh grade.
Functions are held monthly at the Center.
The group held several outstanding events including a sock
hop, dance, beatnik party, Passover affair and skating party. Other
activities were the observance of each member’s birthday, fund
raising for Sisterhood, attending the Charles Palot Club elections
and banquet and contributing to the Center Building Fund.
Sincerest thanks go to chairman Mrs. B. Glossman and her
two able assistants, Mrs. I. Gartner and Mrs. S. Weintraub and
also to the four C.Y.L. sponsors, Evelyn Brauner, Ada Hinda
Block, Marsha Kramer and David Weintraub.

CHARLES PALOT CLUB
Chapter of United Synagogue Youth
President Bruce Yergin
Vice-President Linda Halpern
Vice-President Susan Krivan
Recording Secretary Eleanor Safer
Corresponding Secy Karen Caslar
Treasurer Charles Slott
Another banner year has been completed by this U.S.Y. Chap¬
ter composed of 8th and 9th graders. The membership reached
an all time high of 54 with all members taking part in the bal¬
anced schedule of activities.
Under the able leadership of our advisor and activities direc¬
tor, we began our year with a membership beach party and dance.
Other activities included community service, social, fund raising,
religious, athletic and many other varied programs.
The chapter was represented at the following U.S.Y. conventions: L.T.I. in Hendersonville, Regional Convention in Birming¬
ham and the Regional Board Meeting in Hollywood, Fla.
Another successful year was closed with an Installation Banquet at which time the Youth Chairman, Joe Mizrahi, installed
the newly elected officers.
Our deepest appreciation for their help and understanding during the past year goes to Joel Selber, Bob Fischer and mem¬
bers of the Center Sisterhood.

Tween-agers, the Centers’ youngest social club, is composed of boys and girls in the 5th and 6th grades. Picnics, Hanukah
parties, skating parties, sock hops, bowling and the annual installation banquet were among the monthly activities enjoyed by ap¬
proximately 50 youngsters.
For the new year, plans have been made to limit functions to last year’s most popular activities: bowling, skating, picnics and
athletics.
The Tween-agers’ success is due to the capable leadership of the chairman, Mrs. H. Hart, and her committee, Mrs. J. Ditt-
an, Mrs. Mitchell Schemer and Mrs. Saul Zavon and also the four enthusiastic sponsors from C.Y.L., Janet Goldstein, Rose Zager,
Karen Speizer and Cynthia Stillman. Appreciation also goes to Activities Director, Bob Fischer, for his time and efforts devoted to
us.

Boy Scout Troop 14
Scoutmaster Dr. Harry Yergin
Asst. Scoutmasters Norman Moss and George Glossman
Chairman Troop Committee Morris Leibovitz
Troop Committee Philip Selber, Irvin Ghelerter, Joe Mizrahi,
Shelby Trager, Dr. Sidney Stillman, Joe P.
Safer, David Safer.
Troop 14 has completed one of its most successful years in the history of the Troop.
The Troop continues to grow and now has 50 boys on its roster. Under the leadership of
Scoutmaster Dr. Harry Yergin and Morris Leibovitz, Chairman of the Committee, many
activities were carried out.
At the annual Scout banquet and Charter presentation held during Scout Week the
Troop was awarded its 40th Charter. They were also presented the Plaque and White
Banner for 100% in registration and Boy’s Life subscriptions. A third banner for recruiting
new boys and reaching a set quota for 1959 was also awarded the troop.
On all week-end hikes religious services are conducted by the Scouts themselves Fri¬
day evenings and Saturday mornings.
The troop attended a three-day Ross Allen Camporee at Silver Springs where they
were awarded a certificate for scout spirit and cooperation. At a Mohawk District Camporee
the troop won two blue ribbons, one red and one yellow ribbon, placing two first, one second and one third in all over Scoutcraft,
Scout lore, etc. The Bernard Glossman Award for outstanding Scout was awarded to Senior Patrol Leader Eddie Mizrahi. The Simon
Rothstein Award for Scout making the most advancement was awarded to Leon Yergin. We are proud that three Scouts attained the
rank of Eagle Scout at the District Court of Honor—Bruce Yergin, Jack Fleet and Jay Trussel. Eagle Scout Jerry Safer was awarded
a Palm on his eagle badge.
20 boys attended Camp Echochetee this summer with awards given and advancement made. Scouts Eddie Mizrahi, Bruce Yergin
and David Steinfeld were inducted into the Order of the Arrow. The Scout Committee also honored Dr. Harry Yergin by proposing
him for membership into the Order of the Arrow along with his Scouts.
DR. HARRY YERGIN

Boy Scouts, Cont.
Winners of Ner Tamid Award: Marvin Falis, David Steinfeld, Jerry Safer, Michael Safer, Jack Fleet, Bruce Yergin, Bobby Libby,
Richard Kidder, Charles Moskovitz, Jay Trussell, Lee Sinoff, Charles Slott, Alan Gradman and Marvin Slott, shown with Mr. Morris
Leibovitz and Mr. Louis Safer.
Continued from page 105
During Scout Week Troop 14 awarded the Ner Tamid religious award to 14 boys, which is the largest group of Scouts ever to
receive the Ner Tamid award at one time. Presentation was made by Dr. Flarry Yergin.
Our Troop was represented by six Scouts at the 50th National Jamboree held at Colorado Springs, Colo, in July: Bruce Yergin,
Jerry Safer, Charles Moskovitz, Charles Slott, Eddie Mizrahi and Michael Safer.
Courses in First Aid, swimming, life saving were given during the year. Troop 14 is indebted to Red Cross Instructor Harold
Collins and Frank Woloszynowski who contributed their time so that the Scouts would have a more thorough knowledge of these skills.

CUB SCOUTS PACK 14
44 Cub Scouts of Pack 14 of the Jacksonville Jewish Center were registered at Scout
Headquarters this year. The Pack held a very successful year with varied activities.
Pack meetings are held monthly at the Center. The dens meet weekly under the super¬
vision of Den Mothers and prepare an exhibit for their monthly project on the theme of
the month.
One of the outstanding events of the year was the Father and Son Banquet held with
the Boy Scouts at the Center. Another event of interest was the Outing held in March. The
entire Cub Pack were guests of the Jacksonville Baseball Club again this year.
We are looking forward to another enjoyable year for the boys as well as their parents.
All boys over the age of 8 yrs. are urged to join and are asked to contact the Cubmaster.
C. C. GHELERTER
GROUP OF CUB SCOUTS

Girl Scouting was established to aid young girls to grow into womanhood with loving care and guidance. Dedicated mothers offer
their time and abilities to assure their daughters the lasting friendship, enriching experiences, and the capable leadership which make
for a full and rewarding childhood.
This encourages the growth of maturity which is so necessary for good living and happiness for the rest of their adult lives.
The Center Sisterhood sponsors one Girl Scout Troop and four Brownie troops.

The I960 Center Day Camp was the most popular to date as witnessed by the record breaking attendance of 211 campers.
For the second consecutive year the Day Camp sponsored a little league baseball team composed of the Senior Boy campers.
Another successful record was enjoyed by these veterans of two years.
Highlights of the summer included private use of a pool, color war, our baseball teams and our annual closing Day Camp Show
and Presentation of Awards Night.
Activities that proved most popular this past summer were Jai Alai, paddle ball, soccer, arts and crafts, swimming, horseback
riding and our weekly visits to the lake.
Day Camp Committee: Byron Morris, Chairman; Mrs. Bob Fischer, Mrs. William Rose, Mrs. Seymore Hoffenberg, Bob Fischer,
Mrs. Joe P. Safer, Mrs. Herbert Weinstein, Mrs. Nathan Schneider, Mrs. Tillie Reiser, Dr. Nathan Schneider. Not pictured: Robert
Lasris, Co-Chairman.

THE YEAR’S RECORD
Basketball — The Center sponsored a Men’s All Star Team and a
Boys’ All Star Team.
Softball — Men’s Softball Team played in the Southside Church
League, supervised by the City Recreation Department.
Baseball — Midget Baseball Team represented the Center in the
city-sponsored Willow Branch League and came in second
place.
The pictures on this page indicate the type of program we
offer. In addition to softball, basketball and baseball we have
bowling leagues for both sexes, volleyball, ping pong, weight lift¬
ing and all other forms of gym activity.
Boys’ All Star Basketball Team: Howard Safer, Bruce Witten, Sidney
Drashin, Charles Gusky, Jerry Tobias, Martin Edwards, Steven
Moss, Samuel Greenbaum and Edward Witten. Kneeling, Bob
Fischer, Coach.

Mrs. Club
BOARD OF MR. AND MRS.
The Mr. and Mrs. Club of Jacksonville, now in its seventh year, is one of the community’s outstanding social organizations. With
a membership of approximately 100 couples, the Club attempts to bring about a closer fellowship among Jewish couples in the commun¬
ity. Husbands and wives, serving together, govern the affairs of the organization.
Each month there are two social functions, a small informal get-together and a more elaborate dinner-dance. These social functions
are arranged by committees selected from among the membership for each month’s affairs. During the year the members enjoyed these
social gatherings with the outstanding occasions being the New Year’s Eve Party and the Installation Banquet.
Interested couples are requested to contact any of the officers or members for details concerning membership.

OFFICERS OF AMITY CLUB
The Amity Club has completed three successful years of social activities at the Jacksonville Jewish Center. Its function of bringing
together its members once a month in a friendly and sociable atmosphere has proven to be very successful, both to the old-timers as well
as to the newcomers of our city.
The outstanding affairs of the Club are the New Year’s Eve dinner-dance and the June installation banquet. The theme of each
monthly affair is determined by a committee in charge of its particular month.
This organization is open to married couples between the ages of 31 to 46 with a maximum membership of 85 couples.
The Amity Club holds only one general meeting a year, at which time the officers and board of directors are elected. Regular month¬
ly board of directors meetings are open to the entire membership.
The officers and board members of the Amity Club hope to make this fourth year the biggest and best yet.

EDGAR FELSON
Serving the entire Jewish community of Jacksonville, the Jewish Community Council is the central Welfare Fund and Community
Relations body of the local Jewish community. An association of individuals, synagogues, organizations and social service agencies, the
program encompasses several key activities which give unity, cohesion and coordination to local Jewish communal life.
A community-wide annual Welfare Fund Campaign provides support ¿o significant local, national and overseas organizations. A key
recipient, the National United Jewish Appeal, has expressed American Jewry’s responsibility for the welfare and rehabilitation needs of
Jewish communities the world over for over 20 years. River Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged and the Jewish Welfare Society are
among the local beneficiaries.
The Community Relations Committee, designed to cultivate amicable, positive and cooperative ties with civic, social and church organi¬
zations in the general community, focuses a spotlight on Church-State problems, the influence of Israel as the rapidly emerging center off
vibrant democracy in the Middle East, inter-cultural relations, civil liberties and civil rights and internal affairs within the Jewish com-;
munity itself. When the seeds of overt anti-Semitism erupt, the Council’s Community Relations Committee is thus equipped to act forcefully.
The "Commentator”, the Council’s monthly newspaper, is a major communal project. Involved in presenting communal affairs objec¬
tively are the professional staff in cooperation with the publicity chairman who reflect the community’s diverse groupings.

Honorary President Harry Gendzier
IRVING KLEPPER
The image of a "Home for the Aged" has changed radically in the past decade. The old concept of the "rocking chair" existence has
disappeared and has been replaced by dynamic and vital group living.
The Home has become a miniature society and all the vitality of communal living is manifested by a broad program of enrichment
services. In addition to the basic necessities of day-to-day living, the Home has developed a gamut of activities to encourage participa¬
tion of all residents on an individual choice basis.
The past year has seen the inauguration of new and interesting projects which have been most successful. A Sheltered Work Shop
was organized and sub-contracts were secured for simple assembly jobs from local manufacturers. The participating resident is paid for
work performed. This is limited to his strength and capacity as well as a concern for the conservation of his energies. The dignity of
work as well as earning pocket money is a great morale builder.
Through the cooperation of the Home’s auxiliary, the Ladies Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society, a weekly Senior Citizens Day is held at
the Home to which the older people in the community are invited. Thus they share with our residents an interesting afternoon of recre¬
ational, educational and social activities, as well as a coffee hour. This program has become meaningful to the older person in the com¬
munity because he is able to find stimulating experiences geared to his age and capacity. For the Home residents, it gives the opportunity
of sharing experiences with his contemporaries and thus broadens his community contacts.
These programs, plus the many other activities, such as provided by the Golden Age Club, Residents’ Community Council, a peri¬
odical publication and outings, have created a dynamic atmosphere in the Home where an older person can continue his life to the maxi¬
mum of his capacity and dignity.
HOWARD A. SMITH

Ladies Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society
Hilt
■mmm
íSíiíiiííKÍÍÍ
President j Mrs. Abe Horovitz
Vice-President Mrs. Moe B. Safer
Vice-President Mrs. William Goldman

Executive Secretary Mrs. Frank Diamond
Recording Secretary Mrs. Roy J. Sloat
Treasurer, Dues Mrs. I. Zaslow
Treasurer, Projects Mrs. Louis Witten
The Ladies’ Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society was founded thirty-seven years ago as an
aid society to help the needy of Jewish faith. Even at that time, the founders envisioned a
Home for the Aged in Jacksonville. It was through their perseverance that River Garden
Home for the Aged became a reality fourteen years ago.
When this goal was attained, the role became that of auxiliary to the Home, help¬
ing to nurture its growth and development through the years.
Another successful year has been completed by Ladies Aid, the 618 members playing a special part in serving River Garden in pro¬
viding extra-budgetary needs such as furnishings, redecorating, air conditioning and kitchen and infirmary equipment. Funds for these needs
were raised through Tombola, Donor and Social Printing projects as well as through annual dues and Life Memberships in Ladies’ Aid.
Linens were provided through the Linen Shower. ¡
In the realm of service, Ladies’ Aid sponsors activity and volunteer programs at the Home. Since its inception three years ago, over
14,000 hours of service has been given to the residents through the Gray Ladies program. Ladies’ Aid also sponsors regularly scheduled
entertainment and parties through Special Holidays and Friendly Visitors programs. River Garden residents participate in projects served
by volunteers of the auxiliary, such as the residents’ magazine, the Rhythm Band, ceramic classes and the Hobby Shop.
This year a new project was added at River Garden, a weekly Senior Citizens Day which serves the residents as well as many older
people from the community. They look forward to an afternoon of varied programs including book reviews, lectures on art and music,
films, rhythm band and community singing, followed by an enjoyable coffee hour a nd competitive games.
The members of the Ladies’ Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society renew their pledge of continued devotion and service to River Garden
and its residents in the hope that they can help bring some measure of comfort and meaning to their lives.
MRS. ABE HOROVITZ

DAWSON OPPENHEIMER
An emotional problem threatens the future of a Jewish family and help is needed. An unwed Jewish girl arrives in Jacksonville, em¬
barking on a potentially tragic mission—the bearing of a nameless infant, and there is a pressing demand for guidance that will provide
this girl with the opportunity to face a new future with confidence and assure the innocent child a home and the love of a family. A grown
child is in need of a temporary foster home. An aged and dependent woman at the River Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged must know
that there is purpose to life. A New American must learn to live under circumstances vastly different than those he has known in the past.
A husband and father fell ill, and money was needed to sustain his family during this time of crisis.
The story could go on and on for these are just some of the problems that regularly face the Executive Director and the officers and
members of the Board of the Jewish Welfare Society.
Thus the past year was devoted, as always, to constant attempts to arrive at solutions of problems, endeavoring to alleviate tragedy and
heartbreak and ease suffering. But the year has been marked by more than just the continuation of past duties. An increasingly active
board, through its various committees, dedicated much of its time to self-evaluation. And it is anticipated that there will be a new written
policy statement and a new set of by-laws in effect by the end of calendar I960. The chances seem somewhat brighter that by the time
of this printing, the Agency should have a second full time caseworker sharing with the Executive Director the ever-increasing case load.
Your contributions to the Community Chest-United Fund and the Jewish Community Council make our service possible. (The Chest
allocated $17,844 of our annual budget of $29,827 and the Council allocated up to two-thirds of a case worker’s salary and the cost of
resettlement of New American families). The unusually generous contribution by the Jewish Community of good clothing, furniture and
household equipment, has made it possible for the agency to share these items with clients and with other agencies, thus stretching the
limited funds available for financial help.
May we ask for two other things of those fortunate enough to be able to do them? Our need for foster homes for children and the
aged is urgent! Usually, when there is a need for this ’ last resort” temporary care, the need is pressing. Please help us be ready to meet
the needs. Not only is there the satisfaction of contributing a vital service but there is also financial renumeration. If you are an employer,
please check with us, when you are filling vacant positions. Many skilled local persons check with us to see if we know of vacancies. Oth¬
ers, especially in the older and teen age groups with whom we are working, can best be helped by being given an opportunity to help
themselves.

JACK BECKER
The Zionist Organization of America is the only overall Jewish movement throughout the world which encompasses the totality of
Jewish life with Israel as the central point of gravity.
Before the establishment of the State, our main goal was a political one. Now the cultural phase of our program as well as public
relations, is the foremost task. We continue the struggle for the revival of the Hebrew language wherever a Jew may live. The Zionist
idea permeates the overwhelming majority of Jews everywhere . . . Regardless whether one has Orthodox, Conservative or Reform reli¬
gious affiliation, or perhaps none at all, he may nevertheless be an ardent associate and supporter of the Zionist Movement.
The American Zionists have accomplished a fine and remarkable record of good public relations between the American people and
the State of Israel.
The Z.O.A. welcomes all Jewish men to participate in its activities.

The Jacksonville Chapter of Hadassah is composed of approximately 825 Jewish women
who meet the second Tuesday of each month to serve together in the ranks of the American
Zionist Movement. Hadassah’s members join in efforts to effect the national Hadassah pro¬
gram of health, vocational and education services to Israel and the dedication of our local
women to active participation in the life and expression of American community affairs.
With more than 315,000 members in 1250 chapters and units in the United States and Puerto Rico, Hadassah is the world’s largest
Zionist organization. While its pioneering efforts in medicine and social welfare have permeated every facet of Israeli life, it has con¬
ducted significant activities on the American scene to foster creative and intelligent living through educational programs designed to
stimulate understanding of the Jewish cultural heritage.
To date, Hadassah has raised more than $14,000,000 toward the completion of the new Hadassah Hebrew University Medical Center
which is to be dedicated on August 3, I960 in Israel. This is a tremendous contribution, one that was made possible through the self¬
less devotion of Hadassah’s dedicated members. Together with the Zionist Organization of America, Hadassah supports the Jewish Na¬
tional Fund for the rehabilitation of the land of Israel and Zionist youth work through Young Judaea clubs.
Outstanding events of the coming year will be the opening luncheon on October 11, the Israeli Showcase in December and the
Membership Tea, which will be the featured attraction of the month of January. The spring will be devoted to programs honoring the
donors to Hadassah’s projects. The closing meeting and installation of officers will be held in May.
MRS. A. L. SCHNEIDER

Young Judaea
Young Judaea is a youth organization composed of Senior and Junior groups. In the South summer conventions and winter con¬
claves are held for the Senior and Camp Judaea in Hendersonville, N. C. is held each summer for two weeks for the junior members.
The aims of Young Judaea are fourfold: to promote better understanding of Jewish living and historical background; to foster love
for Zion by aiding in the development of the land of Israel; to actively participate in American affairs of the local community; to fulfill
the ideals of brotherhood in club life and activities.
Miss Sybil Horovitz, City Director of Young Judaea, resigned her duties this spring and is now in Israel, continuing her studies
under the Jewish Agency Year in Israel Program.
The Zionette Senior Club was directed by Susan Margolin the first part of the season. Under her direction many interesting pro¬
grams were presented and she organized the Dance Team which participated at the Conclave in Jekyll Island in December, 1959. This
team presented their annual program of Israeli dances at the Stephen Foster Memorial Festival in White Springs, Fla. Officers of this
club were President, Judy Bossen; Vice-Pres., Elaine Brukner; Secy., Marilyn Berkowitz; Treasurer, Rose Zager.
The Junior Clubs are the Sabrim Club and the Tova Club. Under the direction of Elaine Brukner and Byron Kolitz, these clubs
celebrate Purim with masquerades and games. Another club for girls was organized by Claire Lynn Fleet and held various social events.
Three members of the Zionette Club were awarded scholarships to the national Young Judaean Camp, Tel Yehudah: Judy Bossen,
Marilyn Halpern and Elaine Brukner. All will attend the second session in the month of August and hope to return with increased zeal
to continue their work.
Young Judaea operates under the local Youth Commission which is composed of members of Hadassah and Z.O.A., with Mrs.
Charles Kluglose, as the Chairman.

Of major importance to the local Jewish community is the need for higher education
for its youth. In this field the A. B. Weil Lodge No. 800 of B’nai B’rith is second to none.
To assist deserving students our lodge, as we have done for the past 25 years, is continuing
its scholarship loan fund. A need for additional funds for this purpose prompted a highly
successful fund raising dance at the Beauclerc Country Club. IOEL L GOLDMAN
In the area of recreational activity the Lodge is going all out to promote a bowling
league for its members. State, regional and national tournaments are planned for B’nai B’rith bowlers.
Meetings are held every second and fourth Thursday of the month at the George Washington Hotel at 12:30 noon. Interesting
guest speakers are on the program and all persons are invited to attend.

This past year has been a rewarding and gratifying year for B’nai B’rith women.
The chapter overscribed its goal of allocations by contributing generously to the worthy jjg| |¡|¡ —
services of B’nai B’rith. The National Jewish Hospital, Leo N. Levi Hospital, Bellefaire Chil¬
dren’s Home, Children’s Home in Israel, the Martyrs Forest in Israel, the Henry Monsky and MRS. JACK DORMAN
Alfred M. Cohen Colonies in Israel, the Bogen-Rebencw and Lapidus Forest—an award for
oversubscription. Through the leadership of the Americanism and Civic Affairs Committee B’nai B’rith Women gave woman power to
local drives. They send the daily Jewish newspapers to River Garden residents. The Birthday ladies go each month to Pinecastle School
and give the children a birthday party.
The youth programs reach out to all high school and college youth through B’nai B’rith Girls, A.Z.A. and Hillel groups.
The Anti-Defamation League this year went overboard with the purchase of five of the Dolls for Democracy and the distribution
of B’nai B’rith’s memo books throughout the local schools and churches.
The local chapter had a gain of 98 new members and all of this was helped with the marvelous programs and publicity and full
cooperation of membership, thus winning the district-wide membership award for second largest gain.
In a spirit of friendship B’nai B’rith Women attempts to render a service to mankind in a fine social atmosphere and these achieve¬
ments are possible only through the zealous efforts of the Board and membership.

BARRY SINOFF
The Esquire Club, primarily a social organization, has completed another outstanding year and looks forward to even greater heights
in the future.
Led capably by President Barry Sinoif, Esquire proved during the past year that it was proficient in serving the community in cul¬
tural and religious aspects. An example of this was the Club’s successful trip to the Duval Medical Center to entertain the children. Every
member of the Club volunteered when called upon to help with the Cerebral Palsy Telethon. Aside from the boys’ tireless work through¬
out the night, the club also gave a generous contribution to this worthy cause.
The outstanding social events of the year were the annual Holiday Dance at the Beauclerc Country Club and the Esquire Weekend at
the Beach. The club began the Weekend by turning out in mass for Sabbath services at the Center. The club plans to make this a tradi¬
tional part of our program.
The Esquire Club wishes to thank our sweetheart, Marcia Rothstein, and our Advisor, Charles Zimmerman, for their untiring efforts.

National Council oj Jewish Women
Jacksonville Section
OFFICERS
President Mrs. Lionel Gaines
Vice-President Mrs. Lawrence Jaben
Vice-President Mrs. Melvin Cutler
Vice-President Mrs. Arnold Vandroff
Vice-President Mrs. Albert Levy

Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Benjamin F. Benson
Financial Secretary Mrs. Jack Brookner

Auditor Mrs. Max Meyer
The National Council of Jewish Women is a volunteer service organization which
through a program of service, education and social action helps to meet human needs.
Council’s local activities include recreational activities at the Juvenile Shelter, the Naval
Hospital and at River Garden Home for the Aged. It tested the hearing of over 9,000 Duval
County school children, continued to provide wheelchairs, crutches and hospital beds through
our Medical Loan Shelf and sewed layettes for needy families.
The Jacksonville Section was honored this year by the Northeast Florida Chapter of Social Workers as one of the local organizations
nominated for the Jane Adams Centennial Award in outstanding community services.
Council continues its support to Israel by training leaders for welfare and educational work through scholarships and by sending toys
and clothing to children in Israel. It has also expanded its services by building a new Hebrew Univ. High School.
Council provides a strong, unifying cord of mutual interests and aims for all Jewish women, regardless of a diversity of thinking
and background, so that they may work together in the spirit of Judaism for the good of alJ humanity.
The Jacksonville Section has both a daytime and evening grouo, meeting every third Wednesday and fourth Thursday respectively.

Brandéis University Women s Committee
OFFICERS
President Mrs. Ivan Isaacs
Vice-President Mrs. Philip Bork

Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Herman Miller
Recording Secretary Mrs. Harold Newman

Brandéis University was opened in 1948 as the first Jewish-founded non-sectarian insti- iililHlillllliiM^
tution of higher learning in the western Hemisphere. It was named for the late Supreme
Court Justice, Louis D. Brandéis.
In eight years 90 chapters have been established with a combined membership of 55,000.
The heart of a university, the pulsating center of its cultural life, is its library. The new
Go Id far b Library was dedicated this year. No fund-raising activities are held, as money is f
secured through memberships and contributions to the Book Fund. M . tv A NT tq A Af<s
The Women’s Committee notes the development of Brandéis with pride. Women
throughout the country have made incalculable contributions by fostering good will for Brandéis in their respective communities. The
University has expanded its student body, gathered a distinguished faculty and realized its building program. With the establishment of
Brandéis, Jews in America for the first time have been enabled to serve as host to students of other religious affiliations.

Jewish War Veterans
Meyer Leibovitz Post 199
OFFICERS

The Jewish War Veterans of the United States is the oldest veterans organization in
America. Organized in 1896 under charter of the U. S. Congress, it has served with honor
and distinction throughout the United States and its possessions in the years since its inception.
The aims and objectives of J.W.V. is allegiance to the Constitution of the United States
of America, its flag and institutions; to combat the forces of hate and bigotry and to defend
the honor of our people; to always practice good citizenship and loyalty to our Country. To
perpetuate the memory and honor of our heroic men who have made the supreme sacrifice.
It also gives council and aid to the veteran and his family in time of need.
MAX M. BECKER
It is with pride and glory that our Colors, crowned with the shining Star of David, marches along with the Stars and Stripes, in
all patriotic functions and observances.

Progressive Credit Union
OFFICERS - DIRECTORS
President Moe B. Safer
Vice-President Israel Lipson
Secretary Eugene L. Henry
Treasurer Joseph Cohen
Directors Max Rubin, Benjamin Fink, Louis Berinhout, Edwin Presser
Asst. Directors Samuel Schuster, M. B. Glickstein, Herman Gelman, Aaron Schild
Credit Committee Samuel J. Klepper, Chairman; Louis Moss, Harry Kline
Supervisory Committee Max Becker, Chairman; Isidore Weiss, Herbert Sandler
The Progressive Credit Union, sponsored by the Jewish Progressive Society and char¬
tered by the State of Florida, has continued to grow in its 21 years of operation. It is one
of more than 10,000 Credit Unions in the U. S. The combined membership of these Credit
Unions totals more than 10 million members and has over four billion dollars in assets.
From a very modest beginning of 20 members and $500.00 in assets, the Progressive
Credit Union has grown to over 350 members and more than $200,000.00 in assets. During
its existence it has made over 2000 loans to members, amounting to nearly $3,000,000.00
without a single loss. It is organized to encourage thrift and provide a safe, convenient
medium whereby members can invest and accumulate savings. Being regularly examined
by the Comptroller of the State, the savings of members are safe-guarded in several ways.
Life insurance equal to a member’s savings up to $1,000.00 and Loan Protection Insurance is paid by the Progressive Credit Union
without any cost to the individual member. The climax of each year’s activities comes with the annual meeting held in the Jacksonville
Jewish Center Auditorium. Last year the Board declared a 2l/z% dividend on savings and 10% rebate of interest to borrowing members.
A cordial invitation is extended to anyone who wishes to join.
MOE B. SAFER

MURRAY GLICKSTEIN
The Grey Oaks Lodge located at Switzerland in St. Johns County was organized in 1945 by members of the Jacksonville Jewish
community as a social and recreational club with a limited membership.
The club grounds are located on a picturesque wooded bluff overlooking the widest expanse of the St. Johns River. In addition to
the rustic clubhouse and auxiliary buildings, the club has a 1,000 foot dock, an Olympic-sized swimming pool, bath house and other
facilities.
Although the membership list is limited, there remains an opportunity for members of the community to join and share in the
delightful activities of this unusual recreational organization.
